<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Hybrid electric vehicle</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p>A <b>hybrid electric vehicle</b>  (<b>HEV</b>) is a type of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicle</a> that combines a conventional <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> (ICE)  system with an <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric</a> propulsion system (<a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">hybrid vehicle drivetrain</a>). The presence of the electric powertrain is intended to achieve either better <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> than a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conventional_vehicle" title="Conventional vehicle">conventional vehicle</a> or better performance. There is a variety of HEV types, and the degree to which each functions as an electric vehicle (EV) also varies. The most common form of HEV is the hybrid electric car, although hybrid electric trucks (pickups and tractors) and buses also exist.
</p><p>Modern HEVs make use of efficiency-improving technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake" title="Regenerative brake">regenerative brakes</a> which convert the vehicle's <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> to electric energy, which is stored in a <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_battery" title="Electric vehicle battery">battery</a> or <a href="/wiki/Supercapacitor" title="Supercapacitor">supercapacitor</a>. Some varieties of HEV use their internal combustion engine to generate electricity by spinning an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">electrical generator</a> to either recharge their batteries or to directly power the electric drive motors; this combination is known as a <a href="/wiki/Motor%E2%80%93generator" title="Motor–generator">motor–generator</a>. Many HEVs <a href="/wiki/Idle_reduction" title="Idle reduction">reduce idle emissions</a> by shutting down the ICE at idle and restarting it when needed; this is known as a <a href="/wiki/Start-stop_system" title="Start-stop system">start-stop system</a>. A hybrid-electric produces less emissions from its ICE than a comparably sized gasoline car, since an HEV's gasoline engine is usually smaller than a comparably sized, pure gasoline-burning, vehicle and if not used to directly drive the car, can be geared to run at maximum efficiency, further improving fuel economy. (Natural gas and propane fuels produce fewer emissions.)
</p><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Porsche" title="Ferdinand Porsche">Ferdinand Porsche</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Lohner-Porsche" title="Lohner-Porsche">Lohner-Porsche</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-1">[1]</a></sup> in 1901, hybrid electric vehicles did not become widely available until the release of the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in 1997, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Honda Insight</a> in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2001_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2001-2">[2]</a></sup> While initially perceived as unnecessary due to the low cost of gasoline, worldwide increases in the price of petroleum caused many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automaker" title="Automaker">automakers</a> to release hybrids in the late 2000s; they are now perceived as a core segment of the automotive market of the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  January 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, over 12 million hybrid electric vehicles have been sold worldwide since their inception in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US4mi_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-US4mi-6">[6]</a></sup> As of  April 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Japan ranked as the market leader with more than 5 million hybrids sold, followed by the United States with cumulative sales of over 4 million units since 1999, and Europe with about 1.5 million hybrids delivered since 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US4mi_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-US4mi-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup> Japan also has the world's highest hybrid <a href="/wiki/Market_penetration" title="Market penetration">market penetration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapanHEVcapital_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-JapanHEVcapital-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapHEVshare_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-JapHEVshare-8">[8]</a></sup> In 2016 the hybrid <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> accounted for 38% of new standard passenger car sales, and 25.7% of new passenger vehicle sales including <a href="/wiki/Kei_car" title="Kei car">kei cars</a>. Norway ranks second with a hybrid market share of 6.9% of new car sales in 2014, followed by the Netherlands with 3.7%, France and Sweden, both with 2.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EUpocketbook2014_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-EUpocketbook2014-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Global sales are led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toyota_Motor_Company" title="Toyota Motor Company">Toyota Motor Company</a> with more than 10 million <a href="/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a> and Toyota hybrids sold as of  January 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup> followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honda_Motor_Co.,_Ltd." title="Honda Motor Co., Ltd.">Honda Motor Co., Ltd.</a> with cumulative global sales of more than 1.35 million hybrids as of  June 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honda1mi_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honda1mi-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honda2013_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honda2013-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honda062014_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honda062014-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Corporation" title="Ford Motor Corporation">Ford Motor Corporation</a> with over 424,000 hybrids sold in the United States through June 2015;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ford200K_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ford200K-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sales2012US_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2012US-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sales2013US_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2013US-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sales2014US_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2014US-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sales062015US_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sales062015US-17">[17]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Group" title="Hyundai Group">Hyundai Group</a> with cumulative global sales of 200,000 hybrids as of  March 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, including both <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Motor_Company" title="Hyundai Motor Company">Hyundai Motor Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kia_Motors" title="Kia Motors">Kia Motors</a> hybrid models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyundai200K_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyundai200K-18">[18]</a></sup> As of  January 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, worldwide hybrid sales are led by the Toyota Prius liftback, with cumulative sales of almost 4 million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup> The Prius <a href="/wiki/Nameplate" title="Nameplate">nameplate</a> had sold more than 6 million hybrids up to January 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup> Global Lexus hybrid sales achieved the 1 million unit milestone in March 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lexus1mi_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lexus1mi-19">[19]</a></sup> As of  January 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the conventional Prius is the all-time best-selling hybrid car in both Japan and the U.S., with sales of over 1.8 million in Japan and 1.75 million in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US4mi_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-US4mi-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_powertrain">Types of powertrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Types of powertrain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle_drivetrain" title="Hybrid vehicle drivetrain">Hybrid vehicle drivetrain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1482" data-file-width="2082" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg/220px-2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg/330px-2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg/440px-2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2011_Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid_Limited_--_11-20-2011_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Toyota Highlander Hybrid">Toyota Highlander Hybrid</a> has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Series-parallel_hybrid" title="Series-parallel hybrid">series-parallel drivetrain</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="824" data-file-width="1408" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg/220px-2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg/330px-2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg/440px-2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2008_Saturn_Vue_GreenLine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1380" data-file-width="2440" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg/220px-Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg/330px-Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg/440px-Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet-Silverado-Hybrid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 2005–06 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid">Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a> using the electric motor mainly to power the accessories.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG/220px-BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG/330px-BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG/440px-BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BMW_ActiveHybrid_7_WAS_2010_8967.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/BMW_Concept_7_Series_ActiveHybrid" title="BMW Concept 7 Series ActiveHybrid">BMW Concept 7 Series ActiveHybrid</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a> with an electric motor designed to increase power and performance.</div></div></div>
<p>Hybrid electric vehicles can be classified according to the way in which power is supplied to the drivetrain:
</p>
<ul><li>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_hybrid" title="Parallel hybrid">parallel hybrids</a>, the ICE and the <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> are both connected to the mechanical <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(mechanics)" title="Transmission (mechanics)">transmission</a> and can simultaneously transmit power to drive the wheels, usually through a conventional transmission. Honda's Integrated Motor Assist (IMA) system as found in the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Insight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Civic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honda_Accord_Hybrid" title="Honda Accord Hybrid">Accord</a>, as well as the GM Belted Alternator/Starter (<a href="/wiki/BAS_Hybrid" title="BAS Hybrid">BAS Hybrid</a>) system found in the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu" title="Chevrolet Malibu">Chevrolet Malibu</a> hybrids are examples of production parallel hybrids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-20">[20]</a></sup> The internal combustion engine of many parallel hybrids can also act as a generator for supplemental recharging. As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, commercialized parallel hybrids use a full size combustion engine with a single, small (&lt;20 kW) electric motor and small battery pack as the electric motor is designed to supplement the main engine, not to be the sole source of motive power from launch. But after 2015 parallel hybrids with over 50 kW are available, enabling electric driving at moderate acceleration. Parallel hybrids are more efficient than comparable non-hybrid vehicles especially during urban stop-and-go conditions where the electric motor is permitted to contribute,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-20">[20]</a></sup> and during highway operation.</li>
<li>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Series_hybrid" title="Series hybrid">series hybrids</a>, only the electric motor drives the drivetrain, and a smaller ICE (also called <a href="/wiki/Range_extender_(vehicle)" title="Range extender (vehicle)">range extender</a>) works as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">generator</a> to power the electric motor or to recharge the batteries. They also usually have a larger battery pack than parallel hybrids, making them more expensive. Once the batteries are low, the small combustion engine can generate power at its optimum settings at all times, making them more efficient in extensive city driving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Series-parallel_hybrid" title="Series-parallel hybrid">Power-split hybrids</a> have the benefits of a combination of series and parallel characteristics. As a result, they are more efficient overall, because series hybrids tend to be more efficient at lower speeds and parallel tend to be more efficient at high speeds; however, the cost of power-split hybrid is higher than a pure parallel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-20">[20]</a></sup> Examples of power-split (referred to by some as "series-parallel") hybrid powertrains include 2007 models of <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSHbrid_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-UCSHbrid-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In each of the hybrids above it is common to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking">regenerative braking</a> to recharge the batteries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_by_degree_of_hybridization">Types by degree of hybridization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types by degree of hybridization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">Mild hybrid</a></div>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_hybrid" title="Full hybrid">Full hybrid</a>, sometimes also called a strong hybrid, is a vehicle that can run only on a combustion engine, only on an electric motor, or a combination of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>'s hybrid system, Toyota's <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_Synergy_Drive" title="Hybrid Synergy Drive">Hybrid Synergy Drive</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>/<a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-Mode_Hybrid" title="Two-Mode Hybrid">Two-Mode Hybrid</a> technologies are full hybrid systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_Escape_Hybrid" title="Ford Escape Hybrid">Ford Escape Hybrid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> are examples of full hybrids, as these cars can be moved forward on battery power alone. A large, high-capacity battery pack is needed for battery-only operation. These vehicles have a split power path allowing greater flexibility in the drivetrain by interconverting mechanical and electrical power, at some cost in complexity.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">Mild hybrid</a>, is a vehicle that cannot be driven solely on its electric motor, because the electric motor does not have enough power to propel the vehicle on its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup> Mild hybrids include only some of the features found in hybrid technology, and usually achieve limited <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel consumption savings</a>, up to 15 percent in urban driving and 8 to 10 percent overall cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup> A mild hybrid is essentially a conventional vehicle with oversize starter motor, allowing the engine to be turned off whenever the car is coasting, braking, or stopped, yet restart quickly and cleanly. The motor is often mounted between the engine and transmission, taking the place of the torque converter, and is used to supply additional propulsion energy when accelerating. Accessories can continue to run on electrical power while the gasoline engine is off, and as in other hybrid designs, the motor is used for regenerative braking to recapture energy. As compared to full hybrids, mild hybrids have smaller batteries and a smaller, weaker motor/generator, which allows manufacturers to reduce cost and weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a>'s early hybrids including the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight#First_generation_(2000–2006)" title="Honda Insight">first generation Insight</a> used this design,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup> leveraging their reputation for design of small, efficient gasoline engines; their system is dubbed <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Motor_Assist" title="Integrated Motor Assist">Integrated Motor Assist</a> (IMA). Starting with the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Civic Hybrid</a>, the IMA system now can propel the vehicle solely on electric power during medium speed cruising. Another example is the 2005–2007 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid">Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid</a>, a full-size <a href="/wiki/Pickup_truck" title="Pickup truck">pickup truck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup> Chevrolet was able to get a 10% improvement on the Silverado's fuel efficiency by shutting down and restarting the engine on demand and using regenerative braking. <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> has also used its mild <a href="/wiki/BAS_Hybrid" title="BAS Hybrid">BAS Hybrid</a> technology in other models such as the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_Aura_Hybrid#Aura_Green_Line" title="Saturn Aura Hybrid">Saturn Aura Greenline</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Malibu Hybrid">Malibu Hybrid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span id="Plug-in_hybrids_.28PHEVs.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Plug-in_hybrids_(PHEVs)">Plug-in hybrids (PHEVs)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Plug-in hybrids (PHEVs)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">Plug-in hybrid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG/220px-Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG/330px-Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG/440px-Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bilbao_05_2012_BYD_F3DM_2438.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/BYD_F3DM" title="BYD F3DM">BYD F3DM</a> is the world's first mass-produced plug-in hybrid with an <a href="/wiki/All-electric_range" title="All-electric range">all-electric range</a> of 40 to 60 miles (64 to 96 km).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="1950" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG/220px-DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG/330px-DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG/440px-DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> able to run in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-electric_mode" title="All-electric mode">all-electric mode</a> up to 35 miles (56 km).</div></div></div>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle">plug-in hybrid electric vehicle</a> (PHEV), also known as a plug-in hybrid, is a hybrid electric vehicle with <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable batteries</a> that can be restored to full charge by connecting a plug to an external <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric powersource</a>. A PHEV shares the characteristics of both a conventional hybrid electric vehicle, having an <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>; and of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-electric_vehicle" title="All-electric vehicle">all-electric vehicle</a>, also having a <a href="/wiki/AC_power_plugs_and_sockets" title="AC power plugs and sockets">plug</a> to connect to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>. PHEVs have a much larger <a href="/wiki/All-electric_range" title="All-electric range">all-electric range</a> as compared to conventional gasoline-electric hybrids, and also eliminate the "<a href="/wiki/Range_anxiety" title="Range anxiety">range anxiety</a>" associated with all-electric vehicles, because the combustion engine works as a backup when the batteries are depleted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boschert06_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Boschert06-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HotFlat_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-HotFlat-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese battery manufacturer and automaker <a href="/wiki/BYD_Auto" title="BYD Auto">BYD Auto</a> released the <a href="/wiki/BYD_F3DM" title="BYD F3DM">F3DM</a> PHEV-62 (PHEV-100 km) <a href="/wiki/Hatchback" title="Hatchback">hatchback</a> to the Chinese fleet market on December 15, 2008, for 149,800 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_yuan" title="Chinese yuan">yuan</a> (US $22,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F3DM_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-F3DM-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bwf3dm_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-bwf3dm-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> launched the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> series plug-in in December 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstRetailDelivery_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FirstRetailDelivery-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sales2010_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sales2010-29">[29]</a></sup> At the time, the Volt displaced the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> as the most <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel-efficient car</a> sold in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters112410_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters112410-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCCEPAVolt_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCCEPAVolt-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  December 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volt/Ampera_family" title="Volt/Ampera family">Volt/Ampera family</a> is the world's all-time best-selling plug-in hybrid car, with global sales totaling about 134,500 units since its inception, including over 10,000 Opel/Vauxhall Amperas sold in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Top10PEVs2016_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Top10PEVs2016-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Top10Global062016_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Top10Global062016-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Outlander_P-HEV" title="Mitsubishi Outlander P-HEV">Mitsubishi Outlander P-HEV</a> ranks second with about 119,500 units delivered worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Top10PEVs2016_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Top10PEVs2016-32">[32]</a></sup> Third is the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid" title="Toyota Prius Plug-in Hybrid">Toyota Prius Plug-in Hybrid</a> with cumulative global sales of 79,300 units at the end of January 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMC10miHEVs_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-TMC10miHEVs-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_plug-in_hybrids" title="History of plug-in hybrids">History of plug-in hybrids</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_days">Early days</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early days">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lohner_Porsche.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1021" data-file-width="1530" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lohner_Porsche.jpg/220px-Lohner_Porsche.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lohner_Porsche.jpg/330px-Lohner_Porsche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lohner_Porsche.jpg/440px-Lohner_Porsche.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lohner_Porsche.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lohner-Porsche_Mixte_Hybrid" title="Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid">Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid</a> was the first gasoline-electric hybrid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>William H. Patton filed a patent application for a gasoline-electric hybrid rail-car propulsion system in early 1889, and for a similar hybrid boat propulsion system in mid 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>  He went on to test and market the Patton Motor Car, a gas-electric hybrid system used to drive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tram_cars" title="Tram cars">tram cars</a> and small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybrid_locomotive" title="Hybrid locomotive">locomotives</a>.  A gasoline engine drove a generator that served to charge a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead_acid_battery" title="Lead acid battery">lead acid battery</a> in parallel with the traction motors.  A conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Series-parallel_control" title="Series-parallel control">series-parallel controller</a> was used for the <a href="/wiki/Traction_motor" title="Traction motor">traction motors</a>.  A prototype was built in 1889, an experimental tram car was run in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pullman,_Illinois" title="Pullman, Illinois">Pullman, Illinois</a> in 1891, and a production locomotive was sold to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Street_railway" title="Street railway">street railway</a> company in <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Falls,_Iowa" title="Cedar Falls, Iowa">Cedar Falls, Iowa</a> in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1896, the Armstrong Phaeton was developed by Harry E. Dey and built by the Armstrong Company of Bridgeport, CT for the Roger Mechanical Carriage Company. Though there were steam, electric, and internal combustion vehicles introduced in the early days, the Armstrong Phaeton was innovative with many firsts. Not only did it have a gasoline powered 6.5-liter, two-cylinder engine, but also a dynamo flywheel connected to an onboard battery.  The dynamo and regenerative braking were used to charge the battery. Its electric starter was used 16 years before Cadillac's. The dynamo also provided ignition spark and powered the electric lamps. The Phaeton also had the first semi-automatic transmission (no manual clutch). The exhaust system was an integrated structural component of the vehicle.  The Armstrong Phaeton's motor was too powerful; the torque damaged the carriage wheels repeatedly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>
In 1900, while employed at Lohner Coach Factory, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Porsche" title="Ferdinand Porsche">Ferdinand Porsche</a> developed the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lohner-Porsche_Mixte_Hybrid" title="Lohner-Porsche Mixte Hybrid">Mixte</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> a 4WD series-hybrid version of "<i>System Lohner-Porsche</i>" electric carriage that previously appeared in <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">1900 Paris World Fair</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> George Fischer sold hybrid buses to England in 1901; Knight Neftal produced a racing hybrid in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engines_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-engines-40">[40]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pieper-patent-fig1.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="610" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Pieper-patent-fig1.gif/220px-Pieper-patent-fig1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Pieper-patent-fig1.gif/330px-Pieper-patent-fig1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Pieper-patent-fig1.gif/440px-Pieper-patent-fig1.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pieper-patent-fig1.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Figure 1 of Henri Pieper's 1905 Hybrid Vehicle Patent Application.</div></div></div><p>In 1905, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Pieper" title="Henri Pieper">Henri Pieper</a> of Germany/Belgium introduced a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicle</a> with an electric motor/generator, batteries, and a small gasoline engine.  It used the electric motor to charge its batteries at cruise speed and used both motors to accelerate or climb a hill. The Pieper factory was taken over by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperia_(car)" title="Imperia (car)">Imperia</a>, after Pieper died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Victor_Wouk_p70-74_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Victor_Wouk_p70-74-41">[41]</a></sup> The 1915 <i>Dual Power</i>, made by the <a href="/wiki/Woods_Motor_Vehicle" title="Woods Motor Vehicle">Woods Motor Vehicle</a> electric car maker, had a four-cylinder <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">ICE</a> and an electric motor. Below 15 mph (24 km/h) the electric motor alone drove the vehicle, drawing power from a battery pack, and above this speed the "main" engine cut in to take the car up to its 35 mph (56 km/h) top speed. About 600 were made up to 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaulieu_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaulieu-42">[42]</a></sup> The Woods hybrid was a commercial failure, proving to be too slow for its price, and too difficult to service. In England, the prototype <a href="/wiki/Lanchester_petrol-electric_car" title="Lanchester petrol-electric car">Lanchester petrol-electric car</a> was made in 1927. It was not a success, but the vehicle is on display in <a href="/wiki/Thinktank,_Birmingham_Science_Museum" title="Thinktank, Birmingham Science Museum">Thinktank, Birmingham Science Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMT106_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMT106-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>'s 1928 <a href="/wiki/Experimental_Motorized_Force" title="Experimental Motorized Force">Experimental Motorized Force</a> tested a gasoline-electric bus in a truck convoy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1931 <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Erich_Gaichen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Erich Gaichen (page does not exist)">Erich Gaichen</a> invented and drove from Altenburg to Berlin a 1/2 horsepower electric car containing features later incorporated into hybrid cars. Its maximum speed was 25 miles per hour (40 km/h), but it was licensed by the Motor Transport Office, taxed by the German Revenue Department and patented by the German Reichs-Patent Amt. The car battery was <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake" title="Regenerative brake">re-charged by the motor</a> when the car went downhill. Additional power to charge the battery was provided by a cylinder of compressed air which was re-charged by small air pumps activated by vibrations of the chassis and the brakes and by igniting oxyhydrogen gas. No production beyond the prototype was reported.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG/220px-Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG/330px-Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG/440px-Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panzerjager_Tiger_P_Aberdeen_Proving_Grounds.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Porsche's <i>Elefant</i> tank destroyer, restored for museum display</div></div></div>
<p>During the Second World War, Ferdinand Porsche sought to use his firm's experience in hybrid drivetrain design for powering armored fighting vehicles for Nazi Germany.  A series of designs, starting with the <a href="/wiki/VK_3001_(P)" title="VK 3001 (P)">VK 3001 (P)</a>, the unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/VK_4501_(P)" title="VK 4501 (P)">VK 4501 (P)</a> heavy tank prototype (which became the <i><a href="/wiki/Elefant" title="Elefant">Elefant</a></i> tank destroyer) and concluding with the heaviest armored fighting vehicle ever prototyped, the <a href="/wiki/Panzer_VIII_Maus" title="Panzer VIII Maus">Panzerkampfwagen <i>Maus</i></a> of nearly 190 tonnes in weight, were just two examples of a number of planned Wehrmacht "weapons systems" (including <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf_Fw_191#Failure_and_end_of_program" title="Focke-Wulf Fw 191">the highly-"electrified" subsystems on the Fw 191 bomber project</a>), crippled in their development by the then-substandard supplies of <a href="/wiki/Copper_conductor" title="Copper conductor">electrical-grade copper</a>, required for the electric final drives on Porsche's armored fighting vehicle powertrain designs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predecessors_of_present_technology">Predecessors of present technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Predecessors of present technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake" title="Regenerative brake">regenerative braking system</a>, a core design concept of most modern production HEVs, was developed in 1967 for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Motors" title="American Motors">American Motors</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amitron" title="Amitron">Amitron</a> and called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_Regeneration_Brake" title="Energy Regeneration Brake">Energy Regeneration Brake</a> by AMC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> This completely <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery</a> powered urban <a href="/wiki/Concept_car" title="Concept car">concept car</a> was recharged by braking, thus increasing the range of the automobile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The AMC Amitron was first use of regenerative braking technology in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>A more recent working prototype of the HEV was built by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Wouk" title="Victor Wouk">Victor Wouk</a> (one of the scientists involved with the <a href="/wiki/Henney_Kilowatt" title="Henney Kilowatt">Henney Kilowatt</a>, the first transistor-based electric car). Wouk's work with HEVs in the 1960s and 1970s earned him the title as the "Godfather of the Hybrid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Wouk installed a prototype hybrid drivetrain (with a 16-kilowatt (21 hp) <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a>) into a 1972 <a href="/wiki/Buick_Skylark" title="Buick Skylark">Buick Skylark</a> provided by GM for the 1970 Federal Clean Car Incentive Program, but the program was stopped by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) in 1976 while Eric Stork, the head of the EPA's vehicle emissions control program at the time, was accused of a prejudicial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coverup" title="Coverup">coverup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The regenerative brake concept was further developed in the early 1980s by David Arthurs, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_engineer" title="Electrical engineer">electrical engineer</a>, using <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/off-the_shelf" title="wikt:off-the shelf">off-the shelf</a> components, military surplus, and an <a href="/wiki/Opel_GT" title="Opel GT">Opel GT</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Voltage_controller" title="Voltage controller">voltage controller</a> to link the batteries, motor (a jet-engine starter motor), and DC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">generator</a> was Arthurs'. The vehicle exhibited 75 miles per US gallon (3.1 L/100 km; 90 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>) <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">fuel efficiency</a>, and plans for it were marketed by <a href="/wiki/Mother_Earth_News" title="Mother Earth News">Mother Earth News</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1982, <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Karl_Preikschat" title="Fritz Karl Preikschat">Fritz Karl Preikschat</a> invented an electric propulsion and braking system for cars based on regenerative braking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patents.google.com_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-patents.google.com-52">[52]</a></sup> While clearly not the only patent relating to the hybrid electric vehicle, the patent was important based on 120+ subsequent patents directly citing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patents.google.com_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-patents.google.com-52">[52]</a></sup> The patent was issued in the U.S. and the system was not prototyped or commercialized.
</p><p>In 1989, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audi_AG" title="Audi AG">Audi</a> produced its first <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/iteration" title="wikt:iteration">iteration</a> of the Audi Duo (the <a href="/wiki/Audi_100#C3" title="Audi 100">Audi C3 100 Avant Duo</a>) experimental vehicle, a <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in parallel hybrid</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Audi_100" title="Audi 100">Audi 100</a> Avant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quattro_(four_wheel_drive_system)" title="Quattro (four wheel drive system)">quattro</a>. This car had a 9.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt">kilowatts</a> (12.8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_horsepower" title="Metric horsepower">PS</a>; 12.6 <a href="/wiki/Horsepower#Brake_horsepower" title="Horsepower">bhp</a>) <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motor</a> which drove the rear roadwheels. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trunk_(automobile)" title="Trunk (automobile)">trunk</a>-mounted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-cadmium_battery" title="Nickel-cadmium battery">nickel-cadmium battery</a> supplied <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> to the motor that drove the rear wheels. The vehicle's front roadwheels were powered by a <a href="/wiki/List_of_discontinued_Volkswagen_Group_petrol_engines#NF" title="List of discontinued Volkswagen Group petrol engines">2.3-litre</a> <a href="/wiki/Straight-five_engine" title="Straight-five engine">five-cylinder</a> <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engine</a> with an output of 100 kilowatts (136 PS; 134 bhp). The intent was to produce a vehicle which could operate on the engine in the country, and electric mode in the city. Mode of operation could be selected by the driver. Just ten vehicles are believed to have been made; one <a href="/wiki/Drawback" title="Drawback">drawback</a> was that due to the extra weight of the electric drive, the vehicles were less efficient when running on their engines alone than standard Audi 100s with the same engine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Two years later, Audi, unveiled the second duo generation, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audi_100_Duo" title="Audi 100 Duo">Audi 100 Duo</a> – likewise based on the Audi 100 Avant quattro. Once again, this featured an electric motor, a 21.3 kilowatts (29.0 PS; 28.6 bhp) three-phase machine, driving the rear roadwheels. This time, however, the rear wheels were additionally powered via the <a href="/wiki/Torsen" title="Torsen">Torsen</a> centre <a href="/wiki/Differential_(mechanical_device)" title="Differential (mechanical device)">differential</a> from the main engine compartment, which housed a 2.0-litre <a href="/wiki/Inline-four_engine" title="Inline-four engine">four-cylinder engine</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Research and Development was advancing in the 1990s with projects such as the early <a href="/wiki/BMW_5_Series_(E34)" title="BMW 5 Series (E34)">BMW 5 Series (E34)</a> CVT hybrid-electric vehicle <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMWCVTHybridSedan_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMWCVTHybridSedan-53">[53]</a></sup> In 1992, <a href="/wiki/Volvo_ECC" title="Volvo ECC">Volvo ECC</a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/Volvo_Cars" title="Volvo Cars">Volvo</a>. The Volvo ECC was built on the <a href="/wiki/Volvo_850" title="Volvo 850">Volvo 850</a> platform. In contrast to most production hybrids, which use a gasoline piston engine to provide additional acceleration and to recharge the battery storage, the Volvo ECC used a gas turbine engine to drive the generator for recharging.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Bill_Clinton" title="Presidency of Bill Clinton">Clinton administration</a> initiated the <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_a_New_Generation_of_Vehicles" title="Partnership for a New Generation of Vehicles">Partnership for a New Generation of Vehicles</a> (PNGV) program on 29 September 1993, that involved <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford" title="Ford">Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Council_for_Automotive_Research" title="United States Council for Automotive Research">USCAR</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DoE</a>, and other various governmental agencies to engineer the next efficient and clean vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">United States National Research Council</a> (USNRC) cited <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automakers</a>' moves to produce HEVs as evidence that technologies developed under PNGV were being rapidly adopted on <a href="/wiki/Production_line" title="Production line">production lines</a>, as called for under Goal 2. Based on information received from automakers, NRC reviewers questioned whether the "Big Three" would be able to move from the concept phase to cost effective, pre-production <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a> vehicles by 2004, as set out in Goal 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The program was replaced by the hydrogen-focused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FreedomCAR" title="FreedomCAR">FreedomCAR</a> initiative by the <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> administration in 2001,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> an initiative to fund research too risky for the private sector to engage in, with the long-term goal of developing effectively carbon emission- and petroleum-free vehicles.
</p><p>1998 saw the <a href="/wiki/Panoz_Esperante_GTR-1#Q9_HybridPanoz-Zytek" title="Panoz Esperante GTR-1">Esparante GTR-Q9</a> became the first Petrol-Electric Hybrid to race at Le Mans, although the car failed to qualify for the main event. The car managed to finished second in class at Petit Le Mans the same year.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_hybrids">Modern hybrids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Modern hybrids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_hybrid_vehicles" title="List of hybrid vehicles">List of hybrid vehicles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toyota_PRIUS_(NHW10)_front.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1836" data-file-width="2500" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Toyota_PRIUS_%28NHW10%29_front.JPG/220px-Toyota_PRIUS_%28NHW10%29_front.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Toyota_PRIUS_%28NHW10%29_front.JPG/330px-Toyota_PRIUS_%28NHW10%29_front.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Toyota_PRIUS_%28NHW10%29_front.JPG/440px-Toyota_PRIUS_%28NHW10%29_front.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toyota_PRIUS_(NHW10)_front.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius#1997–2001_(model_NHW10)" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius (first generation)</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HondaInsight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1706" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HondaInsight.jpg/220px-HondaInsight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HondaInsight.jpg/330px-HondaInsight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HondaInsight.jpg/440px-HondaInsight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HondaInsight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight#First_generation_(2000–2006)" title="Honda Insight">2000 Honda Insight (first generation)</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1092" data-file-width="2152" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg/220px-2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg/330px-2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg/440px-2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2nd_Honda_Insight_EX_--_05-13-2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight#Second_generation" title="Honda Insight">2010 Honda Insight (second generation)</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1219" data-file-width="2105" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg/220px-2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg/330px-2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg/440px-2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2010_Ford_Fusion_Hybrid_2_--_08-21-2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 2010 <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> was launched in the U.S. in March 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenCar0409_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreenCar0409-57">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Automotive hybrid technology became widespread beginning in the late 1990s. The first <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass-produced</a> hybrid vehicle was the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a>, launched in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in 1997, and followed by the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Honda Insight</a>, launched in 1999 in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2001_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2001-2">[2]</a></sup> The Prius was launched in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and the rest of the world in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tmc_20080515_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-tmc_20080515-58">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius#1997–2001_(model_NHW10)" title="Toyota Prius">first-generation Prius</a> <a href="/wiki/Sedan_(automobile)" title="Sedan (automobile)">sedan</a> has an estimated <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> of 52 miles per US gallon (4.5 L/100 km; 62 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>) in the city and 45 miles per US gallon (5.2 L/100 km; 54 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>) in highway driving. The two-door <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight#First_generation_(2000–2006)" title="Honda Insight">first-generation Insight</a> was estimated at 61 miles per US gallon (3.9 L/100 km; 73 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>) in city driving and 68 miles per US gallon (3.5 L/100 km; 82 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>) on the highway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2001_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2001-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Toyota Prius sold 300 units in 1997 and 19,500 in 2000, and cumulative worldwide Prius sales reached the one million mark in April 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tmc_20080515_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-tmc_20080515-58">[58]</a></sup> By early 2010, the Prius global cumulative sales were estimated at 1.6 million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAToday_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAToday-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-60">[60]</a></sup> Toyota launched a second-generation Prius in 2004 and a third in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFGate2009_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFGate2009-61">[61]</a></sup> The 2010 Prius has an estimated <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a> combined fuel economy cycle of 50 miles per US gallon (4.7 L/100 km; 60 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFGate2009_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-SFGate2009-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audi_A4_Duo" title="Audi A4 Duo">Audi Duo III</a> was introduced in 1997, based on the <a href="/wiki/Audi_A4#B5" title="Audi A4">Audi B5 A4 Avant</a>, and was the only Duo to ever make it into series production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-1">[1]</a></sup> The Duo III used the 1.9-<a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbocharged_Direct_Injection" title="Turbocharged Direct Injection">Turbocharged Direct Injection</a> (TDI) <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engine</a>, which was coupled with a 21 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt">kilowatts</a> (29 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_horsepower" title="Metric horsepower">PS</a>; 28 <a href="/wiki/Horsepower#Brake_horsepower" title="Horsepower">bhp</a>) electric motor. Due to low demand for it because of its high price,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="vague price (February 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> only about sixty Audi Duos were produced. Until the release of the <a href="/wiki/Audi_Q7#Q7_hybrid_(2005)" title="Audi Q7">Audi Q7 Hybrid</a> in 2008, the Duo was the only <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> hybrid ever put into production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HybridCarHist_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-HybridCarHist-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_Hybrid" title="Honda Civic Hybrid">Honda Civic Hybrid</a> was introduced in February 2002 as a 2003 model, based on the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Civic_(seventh_generation)" title="Honda Civic (seventh generation)">seventh-generation Civic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Civic03_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Civic03-63">[63]</a></sup> The 2003 Civic Hybrid appears identical to the non-hybrid version, but delivers 50 miles per US gallon (4.7 L/100 km; 60 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>), a 40 percent increase compared to a conventional Civic LX sedan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Civic03_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Civic03-63">[63]</a></sup> Along with the conventional Civic, it received a styling update for 2004. The redesigned 2004 <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius#2004–2009_(model_NHW20)" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius (second generation)</a> improved passenger room, cargo area, and power output, while increasing energy efficiency and reducing emissions. The Honda Insight first generation stopped being produced after 2006 and has a devoted base of owners. A second-generation Insight was launched in 2010. In 2004, Honda also released a hybrid version of the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Accord" title="Honda Accord">Accord</a> but discontinued it in 2007, citing disappointing sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford_Escape_Hybrid" title="Ford Escape Hybrid">Ford Escape Hybrid</a>, the first hybrid electric <a href="/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicle" title="Sport utility vehicle">sport utility vehicle</a> (SUV), was released in 2005. Toyota and Ford entered into a licensing agreement in March 2004 allowing Ford to use 20 patents<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> from Toyota related to hybrid technology, although Ford's engine was independently designed and built.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In exchange for the hybrid licenses, Ford licensed patents involving their European diesel engines to Toyota.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Toyota announced calendar year 2005 hybrid electric versions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toyota_Highlander_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Toyota Highlander Hybrid">Toyota Highlander Hybrid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexus_RX_400h" title="Lexus RX 400h">Lexus RX 400h</a> with 4WD-i, which uses a rear electric motor to power the rear wheels, negating the need for a transfer case.
</p><p>In 2006, General Motors Saturn Division began to market a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_hybrid" title="Parallel hybrid">parallel hybrid</a>, the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a>, which utilized GM's Belted Alternator/Starter (<a href="/wiki/BAS_Hybrid" title="BAS Hybrid">BAS Hybrid</a>) system combined with a 2.4-litre L4 engine and an FWD automatic transmission. The same hybrid powertrain was also used to power the 2008 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_Aura_Hybrid#Aura_Green_Line" title="Saturn Aura Hybrid">Saturn Aura Greenline</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Malibu Hybrid">Malibu Hybrid</a> models. As of  December 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, only the BAS-equipped Malibu is still in (limited) production.
</p><p>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Lexus" title="Lexus">Lexus</a> released a hybrid electric version of their GS sport sedan, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexus_GS450h#GS_450h" title="Lexus GS450h">GS 450h</a>, with a power output of 335 bhp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The 2007 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toyota_Camry_Hybrid" title="Toyota Camry Hybrid">Camry Hybrid</a> became available in summer 2006 in the United States and Canada. <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a> launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nissan_Altima_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Nissan Altima Hybrid">Altima Hybrid</a> with technology licensed by Toyota in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commencing in fall 2007, <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> began to market their 2008 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-Mode_Hybrid" title="Two-Mode Hybrid">Two-Mode Hybrid</a> models of their <a href="/wiki/GMT900" title="GMT900">GMT900</a>-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Tahoe_Hybrid#Hybrid_vehicle" title="Chevrolet Tahoe Hybrid">Chevrolet Tahoe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Tahoe" title="Chevrolet Tahoe">GMC Yukon</a> SUVs, closely followed by the 2009 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadillac_Escalade_Hybrid#Hybrid" title="Cadillac Escalade Hybrid">Cadillac Escalade Hybrid</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> For the 2009 model year, General Motors released the same technology in their half-ton pickup truck models, the 2009 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Silverado_Hybrid" title="Chevrolet Silverado Hybrid">Chevrolet Silverado</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GMC_Sierra" title="GMC Sierra">GMC Sierra</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Two-Mode Hybrid models.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ford_Fusion_Hybrid" title="Ford Fusion Hybrid">Ford Fusion Hybrid</a> officially debuted at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Los_Angeles_Auto_Show" title="Greater Los Angeles Auto Show">Greater Los Angeles Auto Show</a> in November 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and was launched to the U.S. market in March 2009, together with the second-generation <a href="/wiki/Honda_Insight" title="Honda Insight">Honda Insight</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury_Milan_Hybrid" title="Mercury Milan Hybrid">Mercury Milan Hybrid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenCar0409_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-GreenCar0409-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latest_developments">Latest developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Latest developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2612" data-file-width="4955" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg/220px-2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg/330px-2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg/440px-2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_Honda_CR-Z_Sport-T_i-VTEC_1.5_Front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Honda_CR-Z" title="Honda CR-Z">Honda CR-Z</a> hybrid was launched in Japan in February 2010, followed by the US in August 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCC0210_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0210-72">[72]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<dl><dt>2009–2010</dt></dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyundai_Elantra_LPI_Hybrid" title="Hyundai Elantra LPI Hybrid">Hyundai Elantra LPI Hybrid</a> was unveiled at the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Seoul_Motor_Show" title="Seoul Motor Show">Seoul Motor Show</a>, and sales began in the <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korean</a> domestic market in July 2009. The Elantra LPI (Liquefied Petroleum Injected) is the world's first hybrid vehicle to be powered by an internal combustion engine built to run on <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a> (LPG) as a fuel. The Elantra PLI is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a> and the first hybrid to adopt advanced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium_polymer" title="Lithium polymer">lithium polymer</a> (Li–Poly) batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyundai0709_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyundai0709-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The Elantra LPI Hybrid delivers a <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy rating</a> of 41.9 miles per US gallon (5.61 L/100 km; 50.3 mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>) and CO<sub>2</sub> emissions of 99 g/km to qualify as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super_Ultra_Low_Emission_Vehicle" title="Super Ultra Low Emission Vehicle">Super Ultra Low Emission Vehicle</a> (SULEV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyundai0709_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyundai0709-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_W221#S_400_Hybrid_(2009-)" title="Mercedes-Benz W221">Mercedes-Benz S400 BlueHybrid</a> was unveiled in the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Auto_Show" title="Chicago Auto Show">Chicago Auto Show</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gab0209_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gab0209-75">[75]</a></sup> and sales began in the U.S. in October 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT1009_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1009-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The S400 BlueHybrid is a <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrid</a> and the first hybrid car to adopt a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium_ion_battery" title="Lithium ion battery">lithium ion battery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gab0209_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gab0209-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCC0609_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0609-78">[78]</a></sup> The hybrid technology in the S400 was co-developed by <a href="/wiki/Daimler_AG" title="Daimler AG">Daimler AG</a> and <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenMild_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-GreenMild-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gab0209_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gab0209-75">[75]</a></sup> The same hybrid technology is being used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ActiveHybrid_7" title="ActiveHybrid 7">BMW ActiveHybrid 7</a>, expected to go on sales in the U.S. and Europe by mid-2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In December 2009 BMW began sales of its full hybrid <a href="/wiki/BMW_X6#ActiveHybrid_X6_(2009–)" title="BMW X6">BMW ActiveHybrid X6</a>, while Daimler launched the <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_M-Class#ML450_Hybrid" title="Mercedes-Benz M-Class">Mercedes-Benz ML450 Hybrid</a> by lease only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT1209_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1209-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="981" data-file-width="1501" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG/220px-Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG/330px-Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG/440px-Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toyota_Auris_HSD_Hybrid_Concept.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toyota_Auris_Hybrid" title="Toyota Auris Hybrid">Toyota Auris Hybrid</a> is the first <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass-produced</a> hybrid electric vehicle built in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JustA_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-JustA-82">[82]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Sales of the <a href="/wiki/Honda_CR-Z" title="Honda CR-Z">Honda CR-Z</a> began in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> in February 2010, followed by the U.S. and European markets later in the year, becoming Honda's third hybrid electric car in the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCC0210_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0210-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT0810_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT0810-83">[83]</a></sup> Honda also launched the 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honda_Fit_Hybrid" title="Honda Fit Hybrid">Honda Fit Hybrid</a> in Japan in October 2010, and unveiled the European version, the Honda Jazz Hybrid, at the <a href="/wiki/2010_Paris_Motor_Show" title="2010 Paris Motor Show">2010 Paris Motor Show</a>, which went on sale in some European markets by early 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GCC100810_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-GCC100810-84">[84]</a></sup>
